  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ae808457-4f76-46a6-bea7-e55528b3c4a0'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer10803451517324623226">"رأيت رام الله" كتاب فاز بجائزة نجيب محفوظ للإبداع الأدبي (1997) هل هي رام الله سرّ الإبداع المحقق!! أم أنها الثلاثون عاماً من الغربة أشعلت في القلب الحنين والاشتياق إلى ساكني رام الله!! أم أنه الوطن المحرم المنتظر على مشارف جسر العبور... جسر العودة ذاك الذي سكن في ذاكرة مريد البرغوثي بصرير خشبة، وبضيق مساحته وقصر طوله. هو ذاك الجسر القصير مشت عبره الذاكرة إلى ذاك الأفق ال</span>
  <span id="freeText10803451517324623226" style="display:none">"رأيت رام الله" كتاب فاز بجائزة نجيب محفوظ للإبداع الأدبي (1997) هل هي رام الله سرّ الإبداع المحقق!! أم أنها الثلاثون عاماً من الغربة أشعلت في القلب الحنين والاشتياق إلى ساكني رام الله!! أم أنه الوطن المحرم المنتظر على مشارف جسر العبور... جسر العودة ذاك الذي سكن في ذاكرة مريد البرغوثي بصرير خشبة، وبضيق مساحته وقصر طوله. هو ذاك الجسر القصير مشت عبره الذاكرة إلى ذاك الأفق الرحب المشبع برائحة الأهل والمترع بالصور القديمة الساكنة في الوجدان.<br /><br />مريد البرغوثي فاز بجائزة عبوره ذلك الجسر الخشبي الصغير وكأنه بتجاوزه تمكن من المثول أمام أيامه، وجعل أيامه تمثل أمامه، يلمس تفاصيل منها بلا سبب مهملاً منها تفاصيل أخرى بلا سبب، مثرثراً لنفسه عمراً كاملاً، في يوم عودته ومن حوله يحسبون أنه في صمت عبر الجسر المحرم عليه بعد ثلاثين عاماً، وفجأة انحنى ليلملم شتاته، كما يلمّ جهتي معطفه إلى بعضها في يوم من الصقيع والتلهف. أو كما يلملم تلميذ أوراقه التي بعثرها هواء الحقل وهو عائد من بعيد.<br /><br />وعلى مخدعه في تلك الليلة، ليلة العودة-لملم النهارات والليالي ذات الضحك، ذات الغضب، ذات الدموع، ذات العبث، وذات الشواهد الرخامية التي لا يكفيه عمر واحد لزيارتها جميعاً، من أجل تقديم الصمت والاحترام، وفي غمرة كل ذلك الروح شاحبة، والنفس ذابلة، وسؤال يقفز، ما الذي يسلب الروح ألوانها والنفس أنغامها؟!! وما الذي، غير قصف الغزاة أصاب الجسر؟. لملم مريد البرغوثي كل ذلك ليحكي في كتابه هذا رحلة عذاب فلسطين من خلال أسلوب قصصي شاعري رائع، جسد صدقه الإنساني المعذب والجميل.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10803451517324623226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6993718179906372245">I've read this book based on the recommendation of a fellow booklover ( Thank you Habiba ) & it was enjoyable .<br/>Mourid barghouti , in رأيت رام الله , dwells on the humanitarian , emotional and personal side of the ongoing palestinian tragedy .<br/>After 30 years of exile , he finally returns to his home town ,Ramallah, that had changed beyond recognition. <br/>I teared up several times while reading this intensely autobiographical novel for it made me feel , even for a little while , what's really like to </span>
  <span id="freeText6993718179906372245" style="display:none">I've read this book based on the recommendation of a fellow booklover ( Thank you Habiba ) &amp; it was enjoyable .<br />Mourid barghouti , in رأيت رام الله , dwells on the humanitarian , emotional and personal side of the ongoing palestinian tragedy .<br />After 30 years of exile , he finally returns to his home town ,Ramallah, that had changed beyond recognition. <br />I teared up several times while reading this intensely autobiographical novel for it made me feel , even for a little while , what's really like to be palestinian , to lose your relatives one after one , to lose your sense of belonging and to see your whole life slowly but surely being taken away from you and you're standing there helpless.<br />But if only I had read the English translation of this book ( I saw Ramallah) , I would have loved it even more as I would have been more engaged in the story .</span>

<div id="review_163248454" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163248454" />
    <a title="VivaPalestina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3770001-vivapalestina"><img alt="VivaPalestina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274963339p2/3770001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/163248454?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="VivaPalestina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="VivaPalestina" href="/user/show/3770001-vivapalestina">VivaPalestina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780747569275" href="/book/show/871001.I_Saw_Ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3770001-vivapalestina?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer163248454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15835966283292857321">I had wished to be able to review the book impartially, to review it as others would read it, but I couldn't. Written by a poet, translated by an artist, was it any wonder that I wished I could quote the whole book were it in my own hands?<br/><br/><b>'Displacement is like death'</b><br/><br/>A more painful story I haven't read, one that resounded strongly within and made me shed scorching hot bitter tears for what I had lost through no fault of mine. A sharp sense of acute hurt, betrayal by forefathers who walked away fr</span>
  <span id="freeText15835966283292857321" style="display:none">I had wished to be able to review the book impartially, to review it as others would read it, but I couldn't. Written by a poet, translated by an artist, was it any wonder that I wished I could quote the whole book were it in my own hands?<br /><br /><b>'Displacement is like death'</b><br /><br />A more painful story I haven't read, one that resounded strongly within and made me shed scorching hot bitter tears for what I had lost through no fault of mine. A sharp sense of acute hurt, betrayal by forefathers who walked away from Ramallah and other cities blessing us with the name 'displaced ones', betrayal at the countries that tried to save us and yet were defeated in humility. <br /><br /><b>'..tells me that Ramallah is no longer mine and I will not return to it. The city has fallen.'</b><br /><br />It was no longer him leaving Palestine; it was me, me that had crossed the bridge to Amman, me that was locked out, me that was sweltering in Cairo's stifling heat, me that was waiting at Jordan’s check point, staring at the dried river. I felt transported through time as though this was my own shore of memories I was walking upon, my own pages of emotions I'm flicking through. The writer’s narration has you transfixed, there with him, and the journey back to your current place and seat is long and wearisome. <br /><br /><b>'The stranger is told by kind people: "You are in your second home here and among your kin." He is despised for being a stranger, or sympathized with for being a stranger. The second is harder to bear than the first.'</b><br /><br />I've always been fond of the English verb 'to yearn'. I felt no other language could sum up my emotions so beautifully in one word, I have a yearning to learn French, I had a yearning to walk barefoot upon the shores of Jaffa. Reading this book has made my word pale beyond comparison. If before I yearned to visit Palestine, now I'm left with a yearn-shaped hole needing to be filled with Palestine.<br /><br /><b>'I do not say thank you little bridge. Should I be ashamed in front of you? Or should you be ashamed in front of me?'</b><br /><br /><b>'Being forbidden to return killed him'</b><br /><br /><b>'For the Palestinian, olive oil is the gift of the traveller, the comfort of the bride, the reward of autumn, the boast of the storeroom, the wealth of the family across centuries'</b><br /><br /><b>'The Occupation has created generations of us that have to adore an unknown beloved; distant, difficult, surrounded by guards, by walls, by nuclear missiles, by sheer terror'</b><br /><br /><b>'All those who have been destined to exile share the same features. For an exile, the habitual place and status of a person is lost'</b><br /><br /><b>'We cannot grumble about it as people grumble about their tiresome capitals. Perhaps the worst thing about occupied cities is that their children cannot make fun of them'</b><br /><br />Who would dare to affectionately complain about Palestine? Londoners can generally agree to complain about three things, the weather, the public transport and the government's taxes. When busses go on strike we are all united in ranting against TFL, people that have never spoken two words together will suddenly be united in their morning grumpiness, facebook status' are updated from around the country through phones all sharing one common theme. Palestinians have been deprived from that. Reproach is only for the beloved we say in arabic, Palestine our beloved we cannot reproach or complain because we're too busy struggling to keep it between our hands.<br /><br /><b>This boy...who has seen nothing of Palestine throughout his twenty years, burns to see it like a refugee grown old in a distant camp</b><br /><br /><b>'Some Palestinians wronged Lebanon. The children of the camps pay the price for this everyday. If only all who had wronged Palestine would pay the price too.'</b><br /><br />The unjustice of it all came crashing down upon me while reading this book. My french and russian Jewish friends may wax lyrical about the beauty of 'Israel' while I, supposedly a Palestine, have never set sight on it. Arabic countries like Lebanon and Jordan are preserving our 'Right of Return' by making sure our stay is only temporary. Children born and brought up in Lebanon, marrying in Lebanon, bringing children into Lebanon, they are stamped as 'temporary'. We are still living in this state of temporariness, waiting.<br /><br /><b>'"Should we leave it to the settlers? Everyone should come back from abroad who can"'</b><br /><br /><b>'The fish<br />Even in the fisherman's net<br />Still carries<br />The smell of the sea'</b><br /><br />Reading this book has only confirmed my memories of Palestine. Do not ask me where they came from, but they are there. I remember Palestine, it's ingrained in every cell in my body that breathes, every atom that makes me. For Palestine is no longer merely a land. It lives and breathes in us, she is us and we are her. <br /><br /><b>The pillow is the register of our lives. The first draft of our story that, each night, we write without ink and tell without a sound'</b><br /><br />The journey that I started with Mourid started and ended on my pillow. It was a witness to the laughs I laughed, to the grins I made. It held my tears and wiped them away promising me a return. It shared my angst and frustration, my love and joy. Do not bother telling me that I'm not a Palestinian as my memory and existance denies otherwise. Do not bother telling me that Palestine will come back to me, for Palestine was never lost to us and never will be. I only hope that we will soon be reunited.<br /><br />For those of you that have managed to reach the end, I only say, 'a must read'. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15835966283292857321" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3086815873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3086815873" />
    <a title="Sleepless Dreamer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7614560-sleepless-dreamer"><img alt="Sleepless Dreamer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1431177042p2/7614560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3086815873?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sleepless Dreamer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sleepless Dreamer" href="/user/show/7614560-sleepless-dreamer">Sleepless Dreamer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400032662" href="/book/show/846564.I_Saw_Ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7614560-sleepless-dreamer?shelf=reading-a-book-from-every-country">reading-a-book-from-every-country</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3086815873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8995166404946210489">The other day, a friend from class asked me what I'm reading. Knowing that she's very right wing, I told her about this book and said that I will read every perspective about the conflict. We had a nice conversation about pluralism and such. She was claiming that pluralism leads to a loss of your own narrative while I very much want to believe that a person can contain an understanding of complex and contrasting views. <br/><br/>Anyway, this book tells us about his return to his childhood home thirty year</span>
  <span id="freeText8995166404946210489" style="display:none">The other day, a friend from class asked me what I'm reading. Knowing that she's very right wing, I told her about this book and said that I will read every perspective about the conflict. We had a nice conversation about pluralism and such. She was claiming that pluralism leads to a loss of your own narrative while I very much want to believe that a person can contain an understanding of complex and contrasting views. <br /><br />Anyway, this book tells us about his return to his childhood home thirty years later. I find the conversation about the right of return fascinating. Of course, I also find the fact that descendants of Palestinians are also considered refugees also absolutely interesting. <br /><br />I've always gotten the impression that these Palestinian descendants don't want to actually live here. They want to know their homeland exists the way they dream of it and they want the ability to visit but truly, I wonder how many Palestinians descendants would actually abandon their lives in the states, in Hungary, in Denmark in order to live here? It's even more intriguing when you consider the refugee camps in Jordan and how the Jordanian population depends on Palestinians at this point. <br /><br />This book brings it up a little bit. One character urges Palestinians to come and as a Jew, I couldn't help but think of how ingrained Israel is in our prayers, our religion is a peoplehood, it's a nation and it's so very connected to this territory in a way that perhaps younger generations of Palestinians can relate to through the education that they receive from their (grand) parents who were refugees. <br /><br />In any case, I had hoped to get this insight into the world of someone that left his home to study abroad and abruptly learned that in this time, there was a war and his home as he knew it is gone. That's scary. Most of my dorm floor is international students and I can't imagine how broken they would be if something happened to their home. <br /><br />This is not what this book was. Barghouti (that family is so wild, it feels weird to use his last name) writes with this dreamy style. I often struggled to figure out exactly what he's talking about, if it's the sixties or the nineties. It was very frustrating. I think the translation was also problematic and hey, inshallah in a few years I'll be able to read it in Arabic. <br /><br />Beyond this, the book doesn't have a very collected narrative. It jumps around in such a way that makes it challenging to paint a perspective. I realize that this is written by a poet but come on, this is biographical, you can do better. <br /><br />When it came down to the content, I was fascinated though! As an Israeli living now in 2019, I view things differently. Everyone acknowledges Oslo was a mistake (fun fact, I was embarrassingly old before I realized that Oslo is actually a city and not just a political thing that we all complain about). We grew up celebrating the six day war, seeing it as a victory. We think about the disengagement and the rockets from Gaza and the deaths from the Intifada. <br /><br />There's a part here where he talks about martyrs getting paid by the Palestinian Authority. I've always read this as "The PA pays terrorists". I still read it this way but I think this was the first time I ever stopped and felt like I could see how hard it must be to be a wife of someone that sacrificed themselves for independence (and ahh, every time I walk down the street of my home, I cross next to a bus station where a bus exploded with such a martyr and killed a few people but when you look at the number, more Palestinians died in the intifadas but they were the ones who initiated them and nothing is ever simple or painless here). <br /><br />Of course, Barghouti jumps over the more uncomfortable sides of the story. He doesn't say who started the six day war, what happened in 1948, how Israel has been constantly outnumbered and surrounded, what happened in the intifadas. And in the same vein, he talks a few times about Sabra and Shatila massacres which we learned about in school in passing and not enough. <br /><br />When I was in Taiwan, a statement I heard over and over was, "yes, we want independence but no, I'm not willing to die for that". Of course, this came from the 7ish people I spoke to but still, it was such a difference from the narrative here where everyone is willing to die to get to the political state they want. <br /><br />Barghouti spends a remarkable amount of time observing soldiers and pondering what they're thinking. He seems to want to bring up the Israeli view in order to disagree with it and yet, he absolutely fails at understanding what Israelis feel. <br /><br />This is highlighted in the last chapter when he talks about Rabin's speech. I get it, Barghouti desperately wants to paint Israelis as land hungry villains who thrive on occupying Palestinians and sending their youth to die on battle grounds. It's harder to understand that the occupation was essentially a mistake, a moment of legal confusion. That soldiers are 18 year olds and yes, we've got to acknowledge each other's pain. That Barghouti can speak the truth and Rabin can also speak the truth and neither one of them is misleading. <br /><br />And I think about my friend and how angry she'd be about how Barghouti can see the pain of martyrs and not the pain of soldiers. How frustrated she'd be by how he ignores that this land is (also) ours. <br /><br />The other day I found myself thinking that if I'll ever actually work in politics, my very rambling reviews might show up and haunt me. I hope to grow so much in the next 2.5 years (and maybe more) of studying politics and understand more. <br /><br />Right now, what stands out is how Israelis who talk about peace don't want to talk about being victims while Palestinians who want to talk about peace don't want to talk about violence on their side. Israelis that don't believe in peace refuse to acknowledge Palestinian pain or sympathize with their story. <br /><br />And all of this is a problem because I don't think Palestinians understand that I grew up learning that a woman with a hijab and a big coat can explode in a bus and therefore, if I see one, I should be aware of my surroundings. Is this racism? Absolutely. And yet, this is also something that happened again and again. It is not just racism, it is fear that was learned. It can be unlearned but only if it's addressed in its complexity, not just as racism and not just as a valid concern. <br /><br />And the army is oppressing and inherently violent but I remember what it felt like to drink coffee surrounded by friends on base and how every soldier I saw was an instant friend just because we were both wearing an army uniform. It's never one or the other. <br /><br />There is so so much that I do not understand about Palestinian experiences. We're never going to understand each other if we don't speak earnestly. I'm not interested in conversations that aren't honest anymore which is leading me to be frustrated with every peace meeting ever. <br /><br />I could write more, there's so much to write. This book opened doors about this conflict that I didn't entirely think of before. I want to open all of those doors. Do I recommend this book? Well, if you are prepared for more poetry than politics, sure. <br /><br /><b> what I'm taking with me </b><br />• I understood that he left his home to study in Egypt when 1967 happened, I hope this is true. <br />• I wonder where's the bridge. <br />• guys, I've been putting a ton of time in my Arabic and like I'm slowly feeling an improvement!! <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8995166404946210489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15305591110222549190">Reread for my essay on representation and exile. Just as, if not more, poignant the second time round. <br/><br/>~~~~~~~~~~~~<br/><br/><i>
  <b>Set text for university</b>
</i> <br/><br/>A poignant and poetic story of exile and return by established poet Mourid Barghouti and beautifully translated by Ahdaf Soueif. <br/><br/>My knowledge of the Palestinian tragedy is limited but reading about Barghouti’s return to his home town Ramallah, in Israeli-occupied Palestine, after 30 years of exile moved me in a way I never anticipated. His town has change</span>
  <span id="freeText15305591110222549190" style="display:none">Reread for my essay on representation and exile. Just as, if not more, poignant the second time round. <br /><br />~~~~~~~~~~~~<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Set text for university</b>
</i> <br /><br />A poignant and poetic story of exile and return by established poet Mourid Barghouti and beautifully translated by Ahdaf Soueif. <br /><br />My knowledge of the Palestinian tragedy is limited but reading about Barghouti’s return to his home town Ramallah, in Israeli-occupied Palestine, after 30 years of exile moved me in a way I never anticipated. His town has changed beyond recognition. Rather than rejoicing in his return, he mourns what he’s lost. This story is filled with elegiac meditations on what it is to lose your relatives one after another; to lose all sense of belonging; to feel powerless as your whole life is taken away from you and the eternal power of storytelling. Although at times chaotic when jumping between points in time, I Saw Ramallah is an eye-opening, emotional story of the wrongly displaced, the estranged, and beyond.</span>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer467121728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9339677255879399537">Mourid Barghouti, now an established poet, returns to his hometown in Israeli-occupied Palestine after thirty years of exile. Here are some of the things I learned from this book: That loss is not abstract, it is concrete. The fig tree in the courtyard that is no longer there. The olive oil to dip your morning bread that now comes from a store. The school house wall plastered with political slogans. The dead brother. The friend that no longer speaks to you. The lost love. These absences are real</span>
  <span id="freeText9339677255879399537" style="display:none">Mourid Barghouti, now an established poet, returns to his hometown in Israeli-occupied Palestine after thirty years of exile. Here are some of the things I learned from this book: That loss is not abstract, it is concrete. The fig tree in the courtyard that is no longer there. The olive oil to dip your morning bread that now comes from a store. The school house wall plastered with political slogans. The dead brother. The friend that no longer speaks to you. The lost love. These absences are real. You can touch them. They live and go on living in so many places, so many things. It amazes me how a book like this, so personal, so full of poetry, so without hatred or even anger, can be so powerful. There's no political agenda here. Berghouti simply describes displacement concretely. He makes you feel homeless there, in Ramallah, and in your own life. That's the book's power. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9339677255879399537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1403638801" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1403638801" />
    <a title="Nada" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10508427-nada"><img alt="Nada" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495132731p2/10508427.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1403638801?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nada" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nada" href="/user/show/10508427-nada">Nada</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789774244995" href="/book/show/894934.I_Saw_Ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1403638801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14078151033323663302">Two lessons the "I Saw Ramallah" experience taught me...<br/>1-If you have a great book in your hands, read everyday, don't put it down for a day or two because you're busy; otherwise you'll be missing out on most of the beauty in it.<br/>2-"Poet" isn't a profession, nor is it a title. If you're a poet, you're a poet and it just shows...<br/><br/>From the very first page of the book, I could strongly sense Mourid's poetry in every word (even though I made the horrible mistake of reading the translated version). I c</span>
  <span id="freeText14078151033323663302" style="display:none">Two lessons the "I Saw Ramallah" experience taught me...<br />1-If you have a great book in your hands, read everyday, don't put it down for a day or two because you're busy; otherwise you'll be missing out on most of the beauty in it.<br />2-"Poet" isn't a profession, nor is it a title. If you're a poet, you're a poet and it just shows...<br /><br />From the very first page of the book, I could strongly sense Mourid's poetry in every word (even though I made the horrible mistake of reading the translated version). I could feel poetry flooding every chapter, every memory, every contemplative thought and every joke.<br /><br />And what makes it even more special is that it touches a story that almost every Arab carries around in their hearts wherever they are, whatever their ideologies are, and whether they're aware of it or not. It's the story of the displaced, the estranged within the borders of their homeland, and beyond.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14078151033323663302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Reem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reem" href="/user/show/9455757-reem">Reem</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1162187421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5820051860578805882">I often think of books as gifts, and this novel is the most precious one of all. I am grateful to Mourid Barghouti who has allowed me to see my Palestine in a whole different light, my parents and grandparents' Palestine, the one we read about in history books but never had the opportunity to experience because by the time we'd grown up walls surrounded us and the colors in the country had been muted. <br/>In one passage, Barghouti describes how the world knows Jerusalem only as the "Holy City" and t</span>
  <span id="freeText5820051860578805882" style="display:none">I often think of books as gifts, and this novel is the most precious one of all. I am grateful to Mourid Barghouti who has allowed me to see my Palestine in a whole different light, my parents and grandparents' Palestine, the one we read about in history books but never had the opportunity to experience because by the time we'd grown up walls surrounded us and the colors in the country had been muted. <br />In one passage, Barghouti describes how the world knows Jerusalem only as the "Holy City" and the center of conflict, and no one knows what lies in its alleyways and how the people of the city laugh and love and grieve, much like anyone else. This is what reading this book felt like: a Palestinian story encompassing the ups and downs and the silly way we Palestinians make jokes about our situation just to get through it. This novel is like an inside look at a small fragment of a Palestinian life (or an intertwined web of several lives) and being able to witness it through Barghouti's words is an absolute privilege and pleasure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5820051860578805882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_185236530" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185236530" />
    <a title="Ming" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3962630-ming"><img alt="Ming" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356496857p2/3962630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185236530?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ming" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ming" href="/user/show/3962630-ming">Ming</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400032662" href="/book/show/846564.I_Saw_Ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185236530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7568413163048706724">This is an absolutely beautiful book. Poetic, of course. And also, vulnerable and intimate. His self-awareness of the pain in returning to Palestine after 30 years of exile is profound and expressed in a very accessible way. This very personal story lends insight into a historical event that reverberates today.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_167110029" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/167110029" />
    <a title="Andy Perdue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5157001-andy-perdue"><img alt="Andy Perdue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353476476p2/5157001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/167110029?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy Perdue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy Perdue" href="/user/show/5157001-andy-perdue">Andy Perdue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780307486141" href="/book/show/6988251-i-saw-ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5157001-andy-perdue?shelf=mideast">mideast</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer167110029" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4472123168873231607">Mourid Barghouti's "I Saw Ramallah" is a remarkable book. The displaced Palestinian relates his return to Ramallah and Deir Ghassana in the mid-1990s, 30 years after the Six Day War. While one might expect Barghouti to rejoice in his return to the city he hadn't seen in three decades, in fact it was a mournful experience because of what he'd lost, what he'd missed through those years. In fact, the phrase, "You can't go home again," perhaps has never been more aptly applied than by Barghouti in "</span>
  <span id="freeText4472123168873231607" style="display:none">Mourid Barghouti's "I Saw Ramallah" is a remarkable book. The displaced Palestinian relates his return to Ramallah and Deir Ghassana in the mid-1990s, 30 years after the Six Day War. While one might expect Barghouti to rejoice in his return to the city he hadn't seen in three decades, in fact it was a mournful experience because of what he'd lost, what he'd missed through those years. In fact, the phrase, "You can't go home again," perhaps has never been more aptly applied than by Barghouti in "I Saw Ramallah."<br /><br />I have crossed the Allenby Bridge on a few occasions but never gave it much thought. Barghouti's experience was enlightening because of what the bridge between the West Bank and Jordan represents.<br /><br />This is an important book to read in light of everything that has happened in the Middle East over the past century. It is a perspective many of us do not understand. It is beautifully written and translated and should be considered a vital book to read if one hopes to gain a greater picture of the news we read almost daily.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4472123168873231607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3144862198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3660755891093833780">The author's reflection on his return to his hometown after many years in exile is a helpful and informative lesson in the complexity of contemporary Palestinian life. The writing is less lyrical than I had expected, perhaps because this is a translation, and his frequent name-dropping is distracting, and of no value to readers who will not recognize the names or their significance, if any. The glossary is beneficial. Expanding it would make it even more so.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Atiqah Ghazali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Atiqah Ghazali" href="/user/show/58139353-atiqah-ghazali">Atiqah Ghazali</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58139353-atiqah-ghazali?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2290038863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3401448831682399092">"Did I paint for strangers an ideal picture of Palestine because I had lost it?"<br/><br/>When I first saw this book, it was the only one on the shelf. When I looked closely at the cover, it was stated 'With a Foreword by Edward W. Said.' Without flipping through, this book became my instant purchase of the month.<br/><br/>Mourid Barghouti is a renown poet and his aesthetic, lyrical words is the reason on why it took me quite sometimes to finish this book. Though it was about his return to his hometown in West Bank</span>
  <span id="freeText3401448831682399092" style="display:none">"Did I paint for strangers an ideal picture of Palestine because I had lost it?"<br /><br />When I first saw this book, it was the only one on the shelf. When I looked closely at the cover, it was stated 'With a Foreword by Edward W. Said.' Without flipping through, this book became my instant purchase of the month.<br /><br />Mourid Barghouti is a renown poet and his aesthetic, lyrical words is the reason on why it took me quite sometimes to finish this book. Though it was about his return to his hometown in West Bank of Palestine from a long protracted exile abroad, the author has written it in the most beautiful prose. It was too moving, I'd wish I am a artist so I could draw the sceneries depicted in it.<br /><br />As this is an autobiography, the story was divided to seven chapters which indicated some places he travelled to in Palestine / Israel, although the last one was more about Palestinians' lives in the occupation land or as refugee. The author didn't paint the Israelites with just one brush, he understood the history behind Oslo Accords and the political repercussions of it.<br /><br />For those who always wonder about Israel / Palestine issue, you may want to add this book to your collection. This book rarely touched on religion, so it gave a new, different perspective. Totally recommend this.<br /><br />"The displaced person becomes a stranger to his memories and so he tries to cling to them."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3401448831682399092" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3332879016" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"

<span id="freeTextContainer11718702459975108065">This is hands down my favourite book that I've read in 2020.<br/><br/>Filled with heartbreaking accounts of life as an exile and life under occupation. Along with touching stories of family and perseverance. <br/><br/>(Referring to Ramallah)<br/>"is she waiting to rest her head on your shoulder or is it you who seeks refuge in her strength."<br/><br/>"...the great gift, in particular, of sight.   <span id="freeText11718702459975108065" style="display:none">This is hands down my favourite book that I've read in 2020.<br /><br />Filled with heartbreaking accounts of life as an exile and life under occupation. Along with touching stories of family and perseverance. <br /><br />(Referring to Ramallah)<br />"is she waiting to rest her head on your shoulder or is it you who seeks refuge in her strength."<br /><br />"...the great gift, in particular, of sight. When I discovered the justice and genius of the language of the camera, which presents its view in an amazing whisper, however noisy this view was in fact or in history."<br /><br />"Talk of politics---and trying to guess what will happen next---never ends. It will remain thus for a long time. Politics have entered into the most miniature details of the souls of our men and our woman since the Zionist project started knocking on the glass of our windows with its sharp nails and then on the doors, which it kicked down to enter all the rooms of the house and throw us out into the desert."<br /><br />"The fish,<br />Even in the fisherman's net,<br />still carries<br />The smell of the sea."<br /><br />And a great quote to end on...<br /><br />"Start your story with secondly and the world will be turned upside down."</span>

  <span id="freeText16721704025796690274" style="display:none">3.5 stars. <br /><br />Definitely not a fun read, but important none the less...<br /><br />I'm not a massive fan of memoir usually; it lacks the satisfying structure of fiction. While they were all good at giving an overview of the situation, a personal account has definitely enriched my knowledge of the Palestinian / Israeli conflict. He simply negated to speak of what happened first. Start your story with 'secondly' and the Red Indians are the original criminals and the guns of the white men are entirely the victims [...] Their fighter jets forgive the graves of our martyrs in Beirut. Their soldiers forgive the tendency of our teenagers' bones to break. Israel the victim polishes its hot, red knife with the sheen of forgiveness.'</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2850592778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14147177804241735616">A memoir that moves one with its poetry of exile. Barghouti captures occupation, displacement and oppression in the daily disruption of life's rhythm and that of other displaced or occupied Palestinians. But in his own rhythm and lyrical writing, he validates his community's ability to overcome disruptive violence. Twenty years plus have passed since it's writing, but Barghouti's work is still pertinent to conversations about Palestinian liberation. The English translation is excellent, but I do</span>
  <span id="freeText14147177804241735616" style="display:none">A memoir that moves one with its poetry of exile. Barghouti captures occupation, displacement and oppression in the daily disruption of life's rhythm and that of other displaced or occupied Palestinians. But in his own rhythm and lyrical writing, he validates his community's ability to overcome disruptive violence. Twenty years plus have passed since it's writing, but Barghouti's work is still pertinent to conversations about Palestinian liberation. The English translation is excellent, but I do feel some of the poetry is lost in translation.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2791168510" />
    <a title="Yalin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69846757-yalin"><img alt="Yalin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501350776p2/69846757.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2791168510?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Yalin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yalin" href="/user/show/69846757-yalin">Yalin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400032662" href="/book/show/846564.I_Saw_Ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69846757-yalin?shelf=historic-storytelling">historic-storytelling</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69846757-yalin?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/69846757-yalin?shelf=undergarduate-readings">undergarduate-readings</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2791168510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2024409648866940586">Barghouti's work is a look into the life and recollections of a Palestinian exile, who finally returns home to Ramallah to revisit his childhood memories and the ideal of home left in his mind. It is an exploration of how he reacts to these changes, and reflects on the life that has been forced upon him by the conditions brought upon his homeland and his people. It is a fantastic read on the Palestinian conditions, and a wonderful personal account by a Palestinian poet.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400032662" href="/book/show/846564.I_Saw_Ramallah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68108778-christopher-iacovetti?shelf=autobiography-memoir">autobiography-memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2999652816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4084278351864084173">'Displacement is like death. One thinks it happens only to other people. From the summer of '67 I became that displaced stranger whom I had always thought was someone else' (p. 3).<br/><br/>'But I do know that the stranger can never go back to what he was. Even if he returns. It is over. A person gets "displacement" as he gets asthma, and there is no cure for either. And a poet is worse off, because poetry itself is an estrangement' (p. 4).<br/><br/>'Now here I am looking at it: at the west bank of the Jordan River</span>
  <span id="freeText4084278351864084173" style="display:none">'Displacement is like death. One thinks it happens only to other people. From the summer of '67 I became that displaced stranger whom I had always thought was someone else' (p. 3).<br /><br />'But I do know that the stranger can never go back to what he was. Even if he returns. It is over. A person gets "displacement" as he gets asthma, and there is no cure for either. And a poet is worse off, because poetry itself is an estrangement' (p. 4).<br /><br />'Now here I am looking at it: at the west bank of the Jordan River. This then is the "Occupied Territory"? ... Who would dare make it into an abstraction now that it has declared its physical self to the senses? It is no longer "the beloved" in the poetry of resistance, or an item on a political party program, it is not an argument or a metaphor. It stretches before me, as touchable as a scorpion, a bird, a well; visible as a field of chalk, as the prints of shoes. I asked myself, what is so special about it except that we have lost it? It is a land, like any land. We sing for it only so that we may remember the humiliation of having had it taken from us. Our song is not for some sacred thing of the past but for our current self-respect that is violated anew every day by the Occupation' (pp. 6-7).<br /><br />'When Palestine is no longer a chain worn with an evening dress, an ornament or a memory or a golden Qur'an, when we walk on Palestinian dust, and wipe it off our shirt collars and off our shoes, hurrying to conduct our daily affairs - our passing, normal, boring affairs - when we grumble about the heat in Palestine and the dullness of staying there too long, then we will really have come close to it' (p. 23).<br /><br />'Am I hungry for my own borders? I hate borders, boundaries, limits. The boundaries of the body, of writing, of behavior, of states. Do I really want boundaries for Palestine? Will they necessarily be better boundaries? ... Now I want borders that later I will come to hate' (p. 38).<br /><br />'The Occupation has created generations without a place whose colors, smells, and sounds they can remember; a first place that belongs to them, that they can return to in their memories in their cobbled-together exiles. ... The Occupation has created generations of us that have to adore an unknown beloved: distant, difficult, surrounded by guards, by walls, by nuclear missiles, by sheer terror. The long Occupation has succeeded in changing us from children of Palestine to children of the idea of Palestine' (p. 62).<br /><br />'Since I left the naivetés of childhood behind me I no longer wish to retrieve the dead so that they come back as I have known them in my past or theirs. I do not want to recapture Deir Ghassanah as it was or my childhood in it as it was. I know the meaning of time passing. But this is no metaphysical matter. I know what it means for cities and villages to be under occupation' (p. 69).<br /><br />'I have always believed that it is in the interests of an occupation, any occupation, that the homeland should be transformed in the memory of its people into a bouquet of "symbols." Merely symbols. They will not allow us to develop our village so that it shares features with the city, or to move with our city into a contemporary space. Let us be frank: when we lived in the village did we not long for the city? Did we not long to leave small, limited, simple Deir Ghassanah for Ramallah, Jerusalem, and Nablus? Did we not wish that those cities would become like Cairo, Damascus, Baghdad, and Beirut? The longing always for the new age. The Occupation forced us to remain with the old. That is its crime. It did not deprive us of the clay ovens of yesterday, but of the mystery of what we would invent tomorrow. I did not come here to reclaim al-Abrash's camel. I used to long for the past in Deir Ghassanah as a child longs for precious, lost things. But when I saw that the past was still there, squatting in the sunshine in the village square, like a dog forgotten by its owners - or like a toy dog - I wanted to take hold of it, to kick it forward, to its coming days, to a better future, to tell it: "Run" (pp. 69-70).<br /><br />'You do not rejoice immediately when life presses a button that turns the wheel of events in your favor. You do not arrive unchanged at the moment of joy dreamt of for so long across the years. The years are on your shoulders. They do their slow work without ringing any bells for you. ... What is needed here is slowness. The vibrations of the past will take their time until they calm down and find a form in which to rest. This needs the slowness of a magician. A precious slowness, allowing feelings of comfort and calm to work their way gently within us. These feelings do not form at once or suddenly. Slowness teaches us how to accept the new; to regard it as natural and the way things have always been. We have to live the new intensely and slowly. We learn this together. ... Radwa waited seventeen years for me to come back to our house, and when I came back I brought all those seventeen years with me. And there were seventeen years with her too' (pp. 72-4).<br /><br />'The impossibility of feeling absolute joy in the thing found after losing it was exemplified in my return to Cairo' (p. 76 - contra Proust?).<br /><br />'My relationship with place is in truth a relationship with time. I move in patches of time, some I have lost and some I possess for a while and then I lose because I am always without a place. I try to regain a personal time that has passed. Nothing that is absent ever comes back complete. Nothing is recaptured as it was. 'Ein al-Deir is not a place, it is a time. ... The thorns of the brambles trained our hands and our sides to bleed early when we were children returning home at sunset to our mothers. Do I want to scramble through brambles now? No, what I want is the time of scrambling. 'Ein al-Deir is specifically the time of Mourid as a child' (p. 87).<br /><br />'The places we desire are only times but conflict is over place. The whole story is about place. They prevent you from owning it and so they take from your life what they take. When a journalist asked me what longing meant for me I said something close to this. It is the breaking of the will. It has nothing to do with the mellowness of memory and remembering. Because of the many places that the circumstances of the Diaspora made us live in, and because we so often had to leave them, our places lost their meaning and their concreteness. As though the stranger prefers the fragile relationship and gets edgy if he feels it becoming strong. The vagrant holds on to nothing. The one whose will is broken lives in his own internal rhythm. Places for him are means of transport to other places, to other conditions, as though they were wine or shoes. Life does not allow us to consider repeated uprootings as tragic for there is an aspect to them that reminds us of farce, and it will not allow us to get used to them as repeated jokes because there is always a tragic side to them. Life teaches us to be content with the only fate proposed to us. It tames us. It teaches us to get used to things. The person on a swing gets used to moving in two opposing directions; the swing of life carries its rider no further than its two extremes, farce and tragedy' (pp. 88-9).<br /><br />'Displacements are always multiple. Displacements that collect around you and close the circle. You run, but the circle surrounds you. When it happens you become a stranger <i>in</i> your places and <i>to</i> your places at the same time. The displaced person becomes a stranger to his memories and so he tries to cling to them. He places himself above the actual and the passing. He places himself above them without noticing his certain fragility. And so he appears to people fragile and proud at the same time. ... But the paradox is that strange cities are then never completely strange. Life dictates that the stranger acclimatize every day. ... Life does not like the grumbling of the living. It bribes them with different degrees of contentment and of acceptance of exceptional circumstances. This happens to the exiled, the stranger, the prisoner, and something like it happens to the loser, the defeated, the abandoned. As the eye accustoms itself, little by little, to sudden darkness, they accustom themselves to the exceptional context imposed by their circumstances. If you become accustomed to the exception you see it in some way as natural' (pp. 131-2).<br /><br /><br />'It is enough for a person to go through the first experience of uprooting, to become uprooted forever. It is like slipping on the first step of a staircase. You tumble down to the end' (p. 131).<br /><br />'Writing is a displacement, a displacement from the normal social contract. A displacement from the habitual, the pattern, the ready form. A displacement from the common roads of love and the common roads of enmity. A displacement from the believing nature of the political party. A displacement from the idea of unconditional support. The poet strives to escape from the dominant used language, to a language that speaks itself for the first time. He strives to escape from the chains of the tribe, from its approvals and its taboos. If he succeeds in escaping and becomes free, he becomes a stranger at the same time. It is as though the poet is a stranger in the same degree as he is free' (p. 132).<br /><br />'[Tamim] knows all the stories of Deir Ghassanah, the stories of the guesthouse, and the news of the old men and women. He tells them in their peasant dialect exactly as though he had been born in Dar Ra'd. His sorrowful anger at the cutting down of the fig tree was more than the anger of the whole family. He will not forgive my uncle's poor wife what she did to a tree that he had never seen with his own eyes nor eaten the fruit of, but he cannot imagine Dar Ra'd without it' (p. 136).<br /><br />'All conflict prefer symbols. Jerusalem now is the Jerusalem of theology. The world is concerned with the "status" of Jerusalem, the idea and the myth of Jerusalem, but our lives in Jerusalem and the Jerusalem of our lives do not concern it. The Jerusalem of the sky will live forever, but our life in it is threatened with extinction. We cannot desert it because of the high rents. We cannot grumble about it as people grumble about their tiresome capitals. Perhaps the worst thing about occupied cities is that their children cannot make fun of them. Who could make fun of Jerusalem!' (pp. 143-4).<br /><br />'The Occupation kept the Palestinian village static and turned our cities back into villages. We do not weep for the mill of the village but for the bookshop and the library. We do not want to regain the past but to regain the future and push tomorrow into the day after. Palestine's progress in the natural paths of its future was deliberately impeded, as though Israel wished to make of the whole Palestinian community a countryside for the city of Israel' (p. 147).<br /><br />'So, when Yitzhak Rabin spoke so eloquently of the tragedy of Israelis as absolute victims, and the eyes of his listeners in the White House garden and in the whole world grew wet, I knew that I would not forget for a long time his words that day: "We are the victims of war and violence. We have not known a year or a month when mothers have not mourned their sons." I felt that tremor that I know so well and which I feel when I know that I have not done my best, that I have failed: Rabin has taken everything, even the story of our death' (p. 178).<br /><br />'It is easy to blur the truth with a simple linguistic trick: start your story from "Secondly." Yes, this is what Rabin did. He simply neglected to speak of what happened first. Start your story with "Secondly," and the world will be turned upside-down. Start your story with "Secondly," and the arrows of the Red Indians are the original criminals and the guns of the white men are entirely the victim. It is enough to start with "Secondly," for the anger of the black man against the white to be barbarous. Start with "Secondly," and Gandhi becomes responsible for the tragedies of the British. You only need to start your story with "Secondly," and the burned Vietnamese will have wounded the humanity of the napalm, and Victor Jara's songs will be the shameful thing and not Pinochet's bullets, which killed so many thousands in the Santiago stadium. It is enough to start the story with "Secondly," for my grandmother, Umm 'Ata, to become the criminal and Ariel Sharon her victim' (p. 178).<br /><br />'The Israelis occupy our homes as victims and present us to the world as killers. Israel dazzles the world with its generosity toward us. Rabin said: "Signing the Declaration of Principles is not easy for me as a fighter in the Israeli Army and in its wars. It is not easy for the people of Israel or for the Jews of the Diaspora." The houses built on top of ours gallantly declare their willingness to understand our odd predilection toward living in camps scattered in the Diaspora of gods and flies, as though we had begged them to throw us out of our homes and to send their bulldozers to destroy them in front of our very eyes. Their generous guns in Deir Yassin forgive us the fact that they piled our bodies high at the sunset hour there one day. Their fighter jets forgive the graves of our martyrs in Beirut. Their soldiers forgive the tendency of our teenagers' bones to break. Israel the victim polishes its hot, red knife with the sheen of forgiveness' (pp. 178-9).<br /><br />'The pillow is our white cotton court of law, smooth to the touch, cruel of sentence. When it has received our heads, crowded with joy and contentment and shame, the pillow becomes a conscience. The pillow is our daily Day of Judgment. A personal Day of Judgment for each one of us who remains alive. ... The defeated may claim victory and believe himself. When he puts his head down on his small pillow it tells him the truth, even if he should deny it. I did not win. He says it to himself without opening his mouth. And if he does not dare, the pillow will dare: you did not win. He may appear once again as a victor in public. He may be supported by some. But those some too will feel the cold tremor when alone in the night of their calculated positions and their patched-together support. The worth of life, the assertion of self, a feeling of pride, an adoption of one story rather than another - all these certainties assured by day, in the dust of the crowd, in the fever of competition and conflict, are turned by our pillows into hypotheses. The pillow is the misgivings that demand to be examined mercilessly' (pp. 180-81).<br /><br />'Tonight, with everyone in the house asleep and morning about to break, I ask a question that the days have never answered: <i>What deprives the spirit of its colors? What is it other than the bullets of the invaders that have hit the body?</i>' (p. 182).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4084278351864084173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1850331323" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10932741689875003404">Barghouti is a Palestinian poet who was prevented from returning to Palestine until the mid-90s, when this travelogue/meditation takes place. Its strength is the ambivalence that he records on returning to the villages and towns of his youth. Like Turki’s Exile’s Return, the Palestinian national experience primes the exile for a romantic reunion with the land, but the conditions and the people turn out to be all-too-human, as he repeats “life cannot be simplified.” Ramallah has been under an unj</span>
  <span id="freeText10932741689875003404" style="display:none">Barghouti is a Palestinian poet who was prevented from returning to Palestine until the mid-90s, when this travelogue/meditation takes place. Its strength is the ambivalence that he records on returning to the villages and towns of his youth. Like Turki’s Exile’s Return, the Palestinian national experience primes the exile for a romantic reunion with the land, but the conditions and the people turn out to be all-too-human, as he repeats “life cannot be simplified.” Ramallah has been under an unjust and at times brutal Israeli control since 67: “The long Occupation has succeeded in changing us from children of Palestine to children of the idea of Palestine.” Too idiosyncratic to be the ideal memoir of the Palestinian condition for novices, it nevertheless has stretches of excellent observations on diaspora and homeland. While ending the Occupation should be our main concern, it seems that Jewish/Israeli suffering can indeed be “simplified” and this is ungenerous. Otherwise, a fine read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10932741689875003404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2319400557" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18037632690772530139">To review a book like that is so darn impossible, especially when it is an award winning autobiography and internationally recognized and appreciated. But I will keep the review as simple and as brief as I can because words will fail me to express what I felt deep inside while reading this book. For starters I will write from the perspective of an Arab reader who already formulated her viewpoint of the Palestinian cause and what Palestine means to her. So the book was a reminder of what I know a</span>
  <span id="freeText18037632690772530139" style="display:none">To review a book like that is so darn impossible, especially when it is an award winning autobiography and internationally recognized and appreciated. But I will keep the review as simple and as brief as I can because words will fail me to express what I felt deep inside while reading this book. For starters I will write from the perspective of an Arab reader who already formulated her viewpoint of the Palestinian cause and what Palestine means to her. So the book was a reminder of what I know and the tragedies she witnesses either through online media or written media or first hand experience of Palestinians like that one. But what was refreshing was the honest philosophical contemplations of the author. There is no need to talk about the beauty of the language used because many critics definitely gushed about a lot.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JRMyPGG1hD50FpSJUKL6ev8MfVCFqGMLhdHVtB2rcmWIbPDU67V7XdvxLCTRsJKo8Lw80PnfrZY4HOeOzsYxnQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('yBpAsZNn9AP3jOOvNX6eOhkPFDotFMMzHS0pRcKN1iBlZYJZGWcLYFhrWwK0bPboFr9VulFjDa6g4Bt/EeCV2A==')})
  }

الزمن الفلسطيني، فقط، وهو هنا نهايات القرن السابع عشر والقرن الثامن عشر بأكمله تقريبا، (1689-1775)، بل في بحثها الأعمق عن أسس تشكّل الهوية والذات الإنسانية في هذه المنطقة الممتدة ما بين بحرين: بحر الجليل (طبرية)، وبحر عكا.\n\nذروة ملحمية يرفع بها إبراهيم نصر الله مشروعه الروائي، ومشروع الملهاة الفلسطينية بشكل خاص، إلى موقع شاهق، وهو يكتب ملحمة ذلك القائد (ظاهر العُمَر الزّيداني) الذي ثار على الحكم التركي وسعيه لإقامة أول كيان سياسي وطني قومي حديث في فلسطين، وأول كيان قومي في الشرق العربي. هذا القائد الفريد الذي امتدت حدود (دولته) من فلسطين إلى كثير من المناطق خارجها.\n\nتعبر هذه الرواية التاريخ وتضيئه على نحو باهر بشخصيات حقيقية وأخرى متخيّلة، متنقلة بين فلسطين وسورية والأردن ومصر ولبنان وإسطنبول، عاجنة التاريخ بالقيم الكبرى وأسئلة الحب والموت والقدر والعلاقة مع الطبيعة في أعمق تجلياتها، ومتأملة التاريخ الروحي والميثولوجي لفلسطين، ومعيدة في آن الاعتبار لتاريخ نضالي وطني فلسطيني متألق، لقائد تاريخي فريد، في فهمه لقيم الكرامة والعدالة والتحرر والحق في الحياة، والتسامح الديني       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3121433._">About مريد البرغوثي</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3121433._"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1310245441p3/3121433.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3121433._">مريد البرغوثي
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,782
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2200822838596177634">مريد البرغوثي شاعر فلسطيني ولد في قرية دير غسانة قرب رام الله في الضفة الغربية تلقى تعليمه في مدرسة رام الله الثانوية، وسافر إلى مصر العام 1963 حيث التحق بجامعة القاهرة وتخرج في قسم اللغة الإنجليزية وآدابها العام 1967 وهو العام الذي احتلت فيه إسرائيل الضفة الغربية ومنعت الفلسطينيين الذين تصادف وجودهم خارج البلاد من العودة إليها. وعن هذا الموضوع كتب مريد البرغوثي في كتابه الذائع الصيت رأيت رام الله</span>
  <span id="freeText2200822838596177634" style="display:none">مريد البرغوثي شاعر فلسطيني ولد في قرية دير غسانة قرب رام الله في الضفة الغربية تلقى تعليمه في مدرسة رام الله الثانوية، وسافر إلى مصر العام 1963 حيث التحق بجامعة القاهرة وتخرج في قسم اللغة الإنجليزية وآدابها العام 1967 وهو العام الذي احتلت فيه إسرائيل الضفة الغربية ومنعت الفلسطينيين الذين تصادف وجودهم خارج البلاد من العودة إليها. وعن هذا الموضوع كتب مريد البرغوثي في كتابه الذائع الصيت رأيت رام الله "نجحت في الحصول على شهادة تخرّجي وفشلتُ في العثور على حائط أعلِّق عليه شهادتي". ولم يتمكن من العودة إلى مدينته رام الله إلا بعد ذلك بثلاثين عاماً من التنقل بين المنافي العربية والأوروبية، وهي التجربة التي صاغها في سيرته الروائية تلك<br />متزوج من الروائية المصرية رضوى عاشور أستاذة الأدب الإنجليزي بجامعة عين شمس بالقاهرة ولهما ولد واحد هو الشاعر والأكاديمي تميم البرغوثي<br />له 12 مجموعة شعرية، ومجلد للأعمال الشعرية. حصل على جائزة فلسطين في الشعر عام 2000. ترجمت بعض أشعاره إلى الإنجليزية والفرنسية والإيطالية والألمانية والروسية والبرتغالية. وحاز كتابه النثري رأيت رام الله (1997) على جائزة نجيب محفوظ للآداب وصدر حتى الآن في 6 طبعات عربية في القاهرة وبيروت وكازابلانكا ورام الله. وترجم الكتاب إلى لغات عديدة منها الإنجليزية والفرنسية والأسبانية والإيطالية والهولندية والنرويجية، والبرتغالية والإندونيسية والتركية والصينية. قرأ مريد البرغوثي شعره ونثره في معظم المدن العربية، وفي عواصم ومدن إنجلترا وفرنسا وإيطاليا وبلجيكا وسويسرا والبرازيل وإسبانيا وشارك في عدد كبير من اللقاءات الشعرية ومعارض الكتاب في العالم. وقدم محاضرات عن الشعر الفلسطيني والعربي في جامعات القاهرة و فاس وأكسفورد ومانشستر وأوسلو ومدريد وغيرها. آخر إصدارته: ديوان متنصف الليل، دار رياض الريس، بيروت، 2005 وقد صدرت ترجمته الإسبانية في ديسمبر 2006 بعنوان Medianoche وفي أكتوبر 2008 صدرت ترجمة إنجليزية لأشعلره في كتاب عنوانه Midnight and Other Poems عن دار النشر الإنجليزية ARC Publications <br /><br /> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16083673.Mourid_Barghouti">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="2200822838596177634" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
